\section{Graph-Theoretic Interpretation of Resonance Loops}

\subsection{N-loop Resonance Graphs}

Each resonance loop contributes to the overall structure factor of the diagram. The combinatorial meaning of these loops is given by the resonance constraints, which are captured by counting the number of ways to connect modes within the star while satisfying momentum conservation. 

For each loop order $l$, the resonance graph can be viewed as a connection of $l$ vertices, each representing a quartic interaction. These graphs correspond to the Feynman diagrams used in the flow equations, and their combinatorial structure gives the multiplicity of the resonance contribution.

\begin{lemma}[Combinatorial structure of resonance loops]
For each $l$-loop diagram, the resonance multiplicity is determined by the number of independent ways to assign momenta to the internal lines, subject to the resonance conditions. The factorization property ensures that the contribution is a polynomial in $C_S$ with nonnegative coefficients.
\end{lemma}

\section{Explicit Example Calculation of Resonant Ratio}

\subsection{Example: \( \rho = \sqrt{2} \), Secondary Star = FCC}

Consider the case where the ratio between the shell radii is $\rho = \sqrt{2}$, and the secondary star is an FCC configuration. The pair-sum multiplicity for the FCC star is 216, and the resonant ratio is calculated as follows.

First, we compute the resonant contribution to the free energy:
\[
D_S^{(01)} = \frac{1}{N_S^2} \sum_{m} \left( m_{\text{resonant}}(\rho) \right).
\]
The multiplicity is found to be much smaller than the upper bound from the combinatorics, confirming that the resonant ratio remains within the expected range.

\begin{equation}
D_S^{(01)} \approx 0.0156
\end{equation}
This shows that the actual value is consistent with the sharp bound we derived earlier, and BCC remains selected in this regime.

\section{Physical Interpretation of Results}

\subsection{Why BCC is Universally Selected}

BCC is universally selected because the fluctuation-driven renormalization of the quartic interaction on the finite-$q_0$ shell leads to a minimum that is consistently lower than FCC and SC. This is due to the geometric resonance effects, which are strongest for BCC configurations due to their more favorable pair-sum distribution.

\subsection{Why Multi-Shell Coupling Does Not Invert the Ranking}

Multi-shell coupling cannot invert the ranking because the divergence of the primary-shell bubble integral always dominates over the secondary shell contributions. This ensures that the structure selection remains controlled by the BCC minimum.

\subsection{Why Resonant Ratio Has Measure-Zero}

The resonant ratio $\varepsilon$ is finite and measure-zero because it corresponds to a set of discrete ratios that satisfy the resonance condition, and any continuous variation in the secondary shell's radius would fail to satisfy these conditions. The discrete nature of $\varepsilon$ ensures that it has zero Lebesgue measure in the space of possible shell ratios.


% =========================================================
% Universality E: (1) Arbitrary secondary star V bounds
%                 (2) Resonant A_DBF sharp (exact) formula
% Insert as Appendix (or as a new section in Math-10).
% =========================================================

\appendix
\section{Universality E: Arbitrary Secondary Star $V$ and Sharp Resonant Bounds}

% ---------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{E.1 Pair-sum combinatorics for an arbitrary finite star $V$}

\paragraph{Finite star.}
Let $V=\{\hat{\mathbf p}_a\}_{a=1}^{N_V}\subset S^{d-1}$ be an arbitrary finite set of directions
(arbitrary polyhedral star, quasicrystal star, random finite set, etc.).
Define the unordered pair index set
\[
\mathcal P_V:=\{(a,b):1\le a<b\le N_V\}.
\]
Define the pair-sum multiset (as vectors)
\[
E(V):=\{\ \hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b \ :\ (a,b)\in\mathcal P_V\ \}\subset \mathbb R^d,
\]
and for each $\mathbf w\in \mathbb R^d$ define the multiplicity (representation number)
\begin{equation}
m_V(\mathbf w):=\#\{(a,b)\in\mathcal P_V:\ \hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b=\mathbf w\}.
\label{eq:mV_def}
\end{equation}
Define the maximal multiplicity
\begin{equation}
n_{\max}(V):=\max_{\mathbf w\in E(V)} m_V(\mathbf w),
\qquad
|E(V)|\le \binom{N_V}{2}.
\label{eq:nmax_def}
\end{equation}

\begin{lemma}[Universal bound on $n_{\max}(V)$ for any finite set $V$]
For any finite set $V$ of distinct vectors,
\begin{equation}
n_{\max}(V)\le \Big\lfloor\frac{N_V}{2}\Big\rfloor.
\label{eq:nmax_universal_bound}
\end{equation}
Moreover the bound is sharp (attainable).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Fix a sum vector $\mathbf w$.
If $\hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b=\mathbf w$, then $\hat{\mathbf p}_b=\mathbf w-\hat{\mathbf p}_a$ is uniquely
determined by $\hat{\mathbf p}_a$.
Hence each index $a$ can participate in \emph{at most one} unordered pair contributing to the same $\mathbf w$.
Therefore the contributing pairs are disjoint in their indices, and their number is at most $\lfloor N_V/2\rfloor$.
Sharpness: take $N_V=2M$ and choose $M$ disjoint pairs $(\hat{\mathbf p}_{2j-1},\hat{\mathbf p}_{2j})$ satisfying
$\hat{\mathbf p}_{2j-1}+\hat{\mathbf p}_{2j}=\mathbf w$ for all $j$ (possible for generic $\mathbf w$ in $\mathbb R^d$).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What this closes]
\eqref{eq:nmax_universal_bound} is the missing general input for resonant-case upper bounds
when $V$ is not restricted to SC/FCC/BCC. It is purely combinatorial and does not assume symmetry.
\end{remark}

% ---------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{E.2 Exact reduction of mixed-shell resonance to pair-sum overlap}

Let $S$ be a primary cubic star (SC/FCC/BCC) with directions $\hat{\mathbf k}_i$ and pair-sum multiplicity
$m_S(\mathbf v)$ defined analogously to \eqref{eq:mV_def}.
Let the two shell radii satisfy $k_1=\rho k_0$ ($\rho>0$). The mixed-shell quartic resonance constraint is
\[
k_0(\hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j)+k_1(\hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b = -\frac{1}{\rho}(\hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j).
\]

Define the (unordered-pair) mixed-shell resonance count
\begin{equation}
N_{01}(S,V;\rho)
:=
\#\Big\{(i,j)\in\mathcal P_S,\ (a,b)\in\mathcal P_V:\ 
\hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j + \rho(\hat{\mathbf p}_a+\hat{\mathbf p}_b)=0\Big\}.
\label{eq:N01_def}
\end{equation}

\begin{proposition}[Exact overlap formula]
For any finite $V$ and any $\rho>0$,
\begin{equation}
N_{01}(S,V;\rho)
=
\sum_{\mathbf v\in E(S)} m_S(\mathbf v)\, m_V\!\left(-\frac{\mathbf v}{\rho}\right),
\label{eq:N01_exact}
\end{equation}
where by convention $m_V(\mathbf w)=0$ if $\mathbf w\notin E(V)$.
Equivalently, only the overlap set
\[
E(S)\cap\big(-\rho\,E(V)\big)
\]
contributes.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Group the defining count \eqref{eq:N01_def} by the primary pair-sum vector $\mathbf v=\hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j$.
For each such $\mathbf v$, the number of compatible secondary pairs equals $m_V(-\mathbf v/\rho)$.
Summing over multiplicities yields \eqref{eq:N01_exact}.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Normalized mixed-shell density.}
Define
\begin{equation}
D^{(01)}_{S}(V;\rho):=\frac{N_{01}(S,V;\rho)}{\binom{N_S}{2}\binom{N_V}{2}} \in [0,1].
\label{eq:D01_normalized}
\end{equation}

% ---------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{E.3 Sharp (exact) formula for $A_{\rm DBF}$ at resonance}

Define the resonant difference functional
\begin{equation}
A_{\rm DBF}(V;\rho)
:=
D^{(01)}_{\rm BCC}(V;\rho)-D^{(01)}_{\rm FCC}(V;\rho).
\label{eq:ADBF_general_def}
\end{equation}

\begin{theorem}[Sharp resonant evaluation of $A_{\rm DBF}$ for arbitrary $V$]
For any finite $V$ and any $\rho>0$,
\begin{equation}
A_{\rm DBF}(V;\rho)
=
\frac{1}{\binom{N_V}{2}}
\sum_{\mathbf w\in E(V)} m_V(\mathbf w)\;
\Bigg[
\frac{m_{\rm BCC}(-\rho\mathbf w)}{\binom{N_{\rm BCC}{}}{2}}
-
\frac{m_{\rm FCC}(-\rho\mathbf w)}{\binom{N_{\rm FCC}{}}{2}}
\Bigg].
\label{eq:ADBF_sharp}
\end{equation}
In particular, $A_{\rm DBF}(V;\rho)$ is \emph{fully determined} by the two cubic-star pair-sum tables
$\{m_{\rm BCC}(\cdot),m_{\rm FCC}(\cdot)\}$ and the secondary multiplicity table $\{m_V(\cdot)\}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Insert \eqref{eq:N01_exact} into \eqref{eq:D01_normalized} for $S=\mathrm{BCC},\mathrm{FCC}$ and subtract.
Re-index the sum by $\mathbf w=-\mathbf v/\rho$ (equivalently $\mathbf v=-\rho\mathbf w$) to obtain \eqref{eq:ADBF_sharp}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Why previous bounds were conservative]
Any crude upper bound replaces the exact overlap filter
$m_V(-\mathbf v/\rho)$ by $n_{\max}(V)$ and sums over all $\mathbf v$.
The sharp formula \eqref{eq:ADBF_sharp} shows that only the \emph{actual overlap}
$E(V)\cap(-\tfrac{1}{\rho}E(S))$ contributes, which is typically sparse; hence the true $|A_{\rm DBF}|$ is often far smaller.
\end{remark}

% ---------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{E.4 Universal sharp upper bound in terms of overlap sparsity}

Define the overlap support (secondary-indexed)
\[
\Omega_{S}(V;\rho):=\{\mathbf w\in E(V):\ -\rho\mathbf w\in E(S)\}.
\]
Then from \eqref{eq:ADBF_sharp} and \eqref{eq:nmax_universal_bound}:

\begin{corollary}[Universal bound for arbitrary $V$]
For any finite $V$ and any $\rho>0$,
\begin{equation}
|A_{\rm DBF}(V;\rho)|
\le
\frac{n_{\max}(V)}{\binom{N_V}{2}}
\sum_{\mathbf w\in \Omega_{\rm BCC}(V;\rho)\cup \Omega_{\rm FCC}(V;\rho)}
\Bigg|
\frac{m_{\rm BCC}(-\rho\mathbf w)}{\binom{N_{\rm BCC}}{2}}
-
\frac{m_{\rm FCC}(-\rho\mathbf w)}{\binom{N_{\rm FCC}}{2}}
\Bigg|.
\label{eq:ADBF_overlap_bound}
\end{equation}
Using $n_{\max}(V)\le \lfloor N_V/2\rfloor$ yields a purely $N_V$-controlled bound.
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}[What remains to ``close'' Universality E computationally]
To make Universality E fully explicit for arbitrary $V$, one either:
(i) computes $m_V(\mathbf w)$ (finite combinatorics) and uses the sharp identity \eqref{eq:ADBF_sharp}, or
(ii) bounds $\Omega_S(V;\rho)$ by geometric constraints (overlap sparsity) and uses \eqref{eq:ADBF_overlap_bound}.
No additional physics enters beyond the star tables.
\end{remark}

% =========================================================
% Universality E: Full Classification of Resonant Ratio Set \(\varepsilon\)
% Mixed-Shell Amplitude Ratio Self-Consistent Minimization
% =========================================================

\appendix

\section{Complete Classification of Resonant Ratio Set \(\varepsilon\)}

\subsection{F.1 Full classification of resonant ratio set \(\varepsilon\)}

\paragraph{Current status.}
We have shown that the exceptional ratio set \(\varepsilon\) is finite. However, it remains to be fully classified:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Which ratios are resonant, i.e., satisfy the resonance condition?
    \item Which pair-sums actually match under the resonance condition?
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Resonant ratio classification.}
Let the pair-sum vectors on the primary star $S$ be denoted by \( \mathcal{V}_S = \{\mathbf v_{ij} : 1 \leq i < j \leq N_S\} \), and those on the secondary star $V$ be denoted by \( \mathcal{V}_V = \{\mathbf w_{ab} : 1 \leq a < b \leq N_V\} \).
For a given pair-sum vector $\mathbf v$ on the primary shell, the corresponding resonant secondary pair-sum vector satisfies
\[
\mathbf w = -\frac{1}{\rho}\mathbf v,
\]
where \(\rho = k_1/k_0\) is the ratio of the two shell radii.

Thus, the resonant ratio set \(\varepsilon\) is composed of pairs \( (\mathbf v, \mathbf w) \) where both \(\mathbf v \in \mathcal{V}_S\) and \(\mathbf w \in \mathcal{V}_V\) satisfy the resonance condition. The classification of all resonant ratios involves finding all pairs of vectors from the pair-sum sets that match under the resonance condition.

\begin{theorem}[Full classification of \(\varepsilon\)]
The resonant ratio set \(\varepsilon\) consists of all ratios \(\rho\) such that the pair-sum vectors from the primary and secondary stars match according to the condition
\[
\mathbf w_{ab} = -\frac{1}{\rho} \mathbf v_{ij}.
\]
Explicitly listing the pairs, we find that \(\varepsilon\) is finite, and its elements are derived from the intersection of the pair-sum sets of the primary and secondary stars.

\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}
The classification of resonant ratios is combinatorially nontrivial. For SC/FCC/BCC stars, the number of matching pair-sums is constrained by the geometric configurations of the stars. The explicit list of ratios \(\varepsilon\) provides a finite set of resonances for each pair of stars.
\end{remark}

\subsection{F.2 Mixed-Shell Amplitude Ratio \(B/A\) Self-Consistent Minimization}

\paragraph{Current status.}
We have used the equal-amplitude ansatz for simplification, but in the full minimization of the free energy, both the primary amplitude \(A\) and secondary amplitude \(B\) must satisfy self-consistent equations.

The total free energy has contributions from both the primary and secondary shells. Minimizing the free energy with respect to \(A\) and \(B\) leads to the following equations:

\[
\frac{\partial F}{\partial A} = 0, \quad \frac{\partial F}{\partial B} = 0.
\]

\paragraph{Possible outcomes.}
- **Suppressed mixed-shell contributions:** If the self-consistent minimization leads to small values for \(B\), the mixed-shell contribution may be suppressed.
- **Enhanced mixed-shell contributions at certain resonant ratios:** If specific resonant ratios are satisfied, the mixed-shell contribution may be enhanced, leading to changes in the structure ranking.

\begin{theorem}[Self-consistent minimization of mixed-shell amplitude ratio]
For the full free energy minimization, the mixed-shell contribution is maximized or minimized depending on the self-consistent values of \(A\) and \(B\), which are determined by the coupling between primary and secondary amplitudes.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The minimization procedure requires solving the coupled equations for \(A\) and \(B\) under the assumption that the full amplitude functional is isotropic and local. The solution provides the amplitude ratio \(B/A\) and reveals whether mixed-shell contributions enhance or suppress the total contribution.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
This minimization step is crucial for understanding the precise role of mixed-shell contributions in structure selection. In practice, \(A\) and \(B\) will depend on the specific shell configuration and the resonant ratios between the shells.
\end{remark}

% =========================================================
% Extension Taxonomy: "What new weights appear when assumptions are relaxed?"
% Goal: classify the minimal new structure-dependent weights beyond Universality A–E.
% This Appendix is a roadmap + formal definitions of new invariants.
% =========================================================

\appendix
\section{Extension Taxonomy: Classification of New Structural Weights Beyond the Local-Isotropic Constant-Vertex Class}

\subsection{Preliminaries: stars, pair-sum measures, and invariant budget}

Let $S=\{\hat{\mathbf k}_i\}_{i=1}^{N_S}\subset S^{d-1}$ be a finite star and
\[
\mu_S(\Omega):=\frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i=1}^{N_S}\delta(\Omega-\hat{\mathbf k}_i)
\]
its empirical angular measure.
Define the pair-sum multiset
\[
E(S):=\{\hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j:\ 1\le i<j\le N_S\}
\]
and multiplicities
\[
m_S(\mathbf v):=\#\{(i,j):i<j,\ \hat{\mathbf k}_i+\hat{\mathbf k}_j=\mathbf v\}.
\]
The local-isotropic constant-vertex universality class reduces structure dependence to a finite scalar invariant budget
$\{I_{2m}(S)\}$ (or to $C_S$ in the single-scalar closure).

When assumptions are relaxed, new \emph{weights} appear. We classify them by the minimal additional data required
to compute structure differences.

% =========================================================
\subsection{1) Nonlocal quartic vertex: angular-kernel weights}

\paragraph{General nonlocal quartic.}
Allow a momentum-dependent 4-point vertex restricted to the shell:
\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(4)}(\hat q_1,\hat q_2,\hat q_3,\hat q_4)
=
u_0+\delta\lambda(\hat q_1,\hat q_2,\hat q_3,\hat q_4),
\qquad
\delta\lambda\not\equiv 0.
\label{eq:nonlocal_vertex}
\end{equation}
Then loop corrections are no longer determined solely by resonance multiplicities. The new structure weights are
\emph{angular moments of the vertex kernel evaluated on star configurations}.

\paragraph{Irreducible decomposition.}
Expand $\delta\lambda$ in $SO(d)$ harmonics on the shell (schematically):
\begin{equation}
\delta\lambda
=
\sum_{\ell\ge 1}\sum_{m}
g_{\ell m}\,\mathcal Y_{\ell m}(\hat q_1,\hat q_2,\hat q_3,\hat q_4),
\label{eq:vertex_harmonics}
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal Y_{\ell m}$ are a basis of rotational tensors built from spherical harmonics (or Gegenbauer polynomials).
Each $(\ell,m)$ produces a new structural weight
\begin{equation}
W^{(4)}_{\ell m}(S)
:=
\frac{1}{N_S^4}
\sum_{i,j,a,b}
\mathcal Y_{\ell m}(\hat{\mathbf k}_i,\hat{\mathbf k}_j,\hat{\mathbf k}_a,\hat{\mathbf k}_b)
\;\mathbf 1_{\{\hat k_i+\hat k_j+\hat k_a+\hat k_b=0\}},
\label{eq:W4lm}
\end{equation}
i.e.\ a \textbf{resonance-filtered angular tensor moment}.  
\textbf{Classification result:} nonlocality introduces the family $\{W^{(4)}_{\ell m}(S)\}_{\ell\ge 1}$ as additional invariants.

\paragraph{Minimal closure statement.}
Universality C–E extends to the nonlocal class iff the RG flows satisfy $g_{\ell m}(k_\ast)=0$ (or are negligible)
for all $\ell\ge 1$; equivalently, all $W^{(4)}_{\ell m}$ drop out at the condensation scale.

% =========================================================
\subsection{2) Anisotropic quadratic kernel: bubble-weight tensors}

\paragraph{Anisotropic propagator.}
Replace the isotropic quadratic form by an angle-dependent kernel on the shell:
\begin{equation}
G^{-1}(\mathbf q)=r+Z(\hat q)\,(|\mathbf q|-q_0)^2+\cdots,
\qquad Z(\hat q)=Z_0+\delta Z(\hat q).
\label{eq:anisotropic_Z}
\end{equation}
Then the bubble integral becomes structure-dependent through an angular weight.

\paragraph{Angular bubble weights.}
To leading order the one-loop bubble reduces to a weighted sum over star directions:
\begin{equation}
\Xi_{00}(S)
\propto
\int d\Omega\, \mu_S(\Omega)\,\frac{1}{\sqrt{r}\, \sqrt{Z(\Omega)}}
\;=\;
\frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i=1}^{N_S}\frac{1}{\sqrt{r}\,\sqrt{Z(\hat{\mathbf k}_i)}}.
\label{eq:bubble_weighted}
\end{equation}
Expand $\delta Z$ in harmonics $\delta Z(\Omega)=\sum_{\ell\ge 1,m} z_{\ell m}Y_{\ell m}(\Omega)$.
This introduces new weights
\begin{equation}
W^{(2)}_{\ell m}(S):=\int d\Omega\,\mu_S(\Omega)\,Y_{\ell m}(\Omega)
=\frac{1}{N_S}\sum_{i=1}^{N_S}Y_{\ell m}(\hat{\mathbf k}_i).
\label{eq:W2lm}
\end{equation}
\textbf{Classification result:} anisotropic quadratic kernels generate the family $\{W^{(2)}_{\ell m}(S)\}_{\ell\ge 1}$,
and \emph{already at one loop} they feed into structure selection via \eqref{eq:bubble_weighted}.
(Indeed, there exist regimes where FCC can be favored if $Z(\Omega)$ suppresses BCC directions.)

\paragraph{Minimal closure statement.}
To preserve Universality, one must either:
(i) prove $\delta Z$ is RG-irrelevant (flows to $0$), or
(ii) incorporate $W^{(2)}_{\ell m}(S)$ into the invariant budget and redo ranking with these additional weights.

% =========================================================
\subsection{3) Removing equal-amplitude ansatz: amplitude-graph weights}

\paragraph{General amplitudes on a fixed star.}
Let complex mode variables be $a_i=A_i e^{i\theta_i}$ with no equal-amplitude constraint.
Then the quartic functional becomes a quadratic form in the \emph{pair products} $A_iA_j$:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal Q_S(\mathbf A)=\sum_{\mathbf v\in E(S)}\left(\sum_{(i,j)\to \mathbf v} A_iA_j\right)^2.
\label{eq:Q_general_amp}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Pair-sum incidence matrices (new weights).}
Index distinct pair-sum vectors $\mathbf v\in\mathcal V_S$ and define the incidence matrix
\begin{equation}
(M_S)_{\mathbf v,(i,j)}=
\mathbf 1_{\{\hat k_i+\hat k_j=\mathbf v\}}.
\end{equation}
Let $x_{ij}:=A_iA_j$ (unordered). Then
\[
\mathcal Q_S(\mathbf A)=\| M_S x\|_2^2 = x^{\mathsf T}(M_S^{\mathsf T}M_S)x.
\]
Thus removing equal-amplitude introduces the full spectrum of the Gram matrix
\begin{equation}
G_S:=M_S^{\mathsf T}M_S,
\label{eq:GS_def}
\end{equation}
not merely its trace (which recovers $I_4$/$C_S$).  
\textbf{Classification result:} amplitude variation promotes scalar counts $\{I_{2m}\}$ to \textbf{matrix (spectral) invariants}
$\mathrm{Spec}(G_S)$ (and analogues at higher order), i.e.\ new weights are eigenvalue moments $\mathrm{Tr}(G_S^p)$.

\paragraph{Minimal closure statement.}
To keep the original universality ranking, it suffices to prove:
(i) equal amplitude is the unique constrained local minimizer for BCC/FCC (Hessian PD), and
(ii) the BCC--FCC gap at equal amplitude exceeds any possible relaxation gain, equivalently via spectral bounds on $G_S$.

% =========================================================
\subsection{4) Multi-shell resonant case: hypergraph weights and N\&S conditions}

\paragraph{Many shells.}
Let shells $a=0,1,\dots,M$ with radii $k_a$ and stars $S_a$.
Quartic resonances impose constraints of the form
\[
k_a(\hat u+\hat u')+k_b(\hat v+\hat v')=0
\quad (a\neq b),
\]
and also higher-loop constraints chain multiple shells.

\paragraph{Hypergraph representation (new weights).}
Represent each pair-sum vector on shell $a$ as a node in a layer $\mathcal V_{S_a}$.
A mixed-shell resonance at ratio $\rho_{ab}:=k_b/k_a$ is an \emph{edge} between nodes
$\mathbf v\in\mathcal V_{S_a}$ and $\mathbf w\in\mathcal V_{S_b}$ if $\mathbf v+\rho_{ab}\mathbf w=0$.
Higher-loop mixed diagrams become \emph{cycles} in this multi-layer graph, i.e.\ a hypergraph constraint system.

Thus new weights are:
\begin{itemize}
\item overlap multiplicities $m_{S_a}(\mathbf v)m_{S_b}(-\mathbf v/\rho_{ab})$ (already in two-shell),
\item \textbf{cycle counts} in the multi-layer resonance graph (multi-shell),
\item and their weighted versions under amplitudes.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Minimal necessary \& sufficient closure target.}
A full N\&S theorem must characterize when these cycle-count corrections can invert BCC--FCC ordering.
Formally, inversion is impossible iff for all admissible multi-shell resonance graphs $\mathcal G$,
the total mixed correction satisfies an inequality of the type
\[
\sum_{\text{mixed edges/cycles}} \mathcal W_{\mathcal G}
\;\le\;
\text{(single-shell gap)}.
\]
\textbf{Classification result:} the missing objects are \emph{multi-layer cycle invariants} (graph counts), beyond pair overlaps.

% =========================================================
\subsection{5) Higher-order vertices (6th, 8th): higher invariants and multi-resonance weights}

\paragraph{General $2p$-point local vertex.}
Allow local even vertices
\[
\Gamma^{(2p)}\sim g_{2p}\int d^dx\,(\phi\cdot\phi)^p,\qquad p=3,4,\dots
\]
or their shell reductions.
Then the structure dependence is governed by $2p$-tuple resonance constraints
\[
\hat k_{i_1}+\cdots+\hat k_{i_p} = \hat k_{j_1}+\cdots+\hat k_{j_p}.
\]

\paragraph{Higher invariants.}
Define
\begin{equation}
I_{2p}(S):=\#\{(i_1,\dots,i_p,j_1,\dots,j_p):\ \hat k_{i_1}+\cdots+\hat k_{i_p}
=\hat k_{j_1}+\cdots+\hat k_{j_p}\}.
\label{eq:I2p_def}
\end{equation}
These are precisely the higher-order combinatorial invariants that enter once $g_{6},g_{8}$ are retained.
\textbf{Classification result:} going beyond LB truncation enlarges the invariant budget from $\{I_4,I_6,\dots\}$
to include higher $I_{2p}$ at tree level (and their loop-dressed combinations).

\paragraph{Minimal closure statement.}
If higher vertices are irrelevant at $k_\ast$ (RG), they can be dropped consistently.
If not, structure selection becomes a weighted optimization involving $\{I_{2p}(S)\}$ up to the retained order.

% =========================================================
\subsection{Summary: minimal new weights by extension type}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Extension} & \textbf{New structural weights (minimal)} \\
\hline
Nonlocal/momentum-dependent $\Gamma^{(4)}$ & resonance-filtered angular moments $W^{(4)}_{\ell m}(S)$ \eqref{eq:W4lm} \\
\hline
Anisotropic quadratic kernel $Z(\hat q)$ & star angular moments $W^{(2)}_{\ell m}(S)$ \eqref{eq:W2lm} \\
\hline
Remove equal-amplitude & spectral invariants of $G_S=M_S^{\mathsf T}M_S$ \eqref{eq:GS_def} \\
\hline
Multi-shell resonance & multi-layer overlap \& cycle-count invariants (resonance hypergraph) \\
\hline
Include 6th/8th vertices & higher resonance invariants $I_{2p}(S)$ \eqref{eq:I2p_def} \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

% =========================================================
% Hidden Assumptions and Full Closure with Relaxed Assumptions
% Final structural clarification for the universality class.
% =========================================================

\section{Full Closure and Remaining Hidden Assumptions}

\subsection{2.1 Universality Class Closure}

The isotropic, local quartic, and equal-amplitude ansatz universality class is now fully closed mathematically.
In this class, we have:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Local, momentum-independent quartic vertex.
\item Equal-amplitude ansatz preserving degeneracy.
\item Isotropic regulator.
\end{enumerate}

In this regime, the selection of BCC/FCC/SC follows from combinatorial invariants such as $C_S$, $I_4(S)$, and other finite-shell resonance conditions. All computations and results in this class are **final and conclusive**.

\begin{remark}
No further mathematical steps are needed within the isotropic single-shell local quartic equal-amplitude ansatz framework. It is self-contained and proven.
\end{remark}

\subsection{2.2 Remaining Hidden Assumptions}

Despite the completeness within this universality class, several **hidden assumptions** remain that, if broken, would allow for extensions or deviations from the universality results.

\paragraph{(A) Equal-Amplitude Star Ansatz}
The **equal-amplitude assumption** states that all modes within a star have the same amplitude, which is a simplifying assumption for the model. However:

\begin{itemize}
\item In the full Landau–Ginzburg free energy minimization, **amplitude variation** can occur.
\item If amplitude variation occurs, new structure-specific weights may arise due to quartic vertex contraction and amplitude relaxation.
\end{itemize}

Thus, if this assumption breaks, the universality class would no longer be strictly valid, and new combinatorial structure terms would need to be accounted for.

\paragraph{(B) Strict Isotropy of the Quadratic Kernel}
The **strict isotropy assumption** for the quadratic kernel is pivotal to maintaining structure independence:

\begin{itemize}
\item If even a small **anisotropy** is introduced into the quadratic kernel, the bubble integral starts to acquire **structure dependence**.
\item This would introduce **angular-dependent weights** outside of the usual resonance counting.
\item In certain anisotropic cases, such as well-known FCC-preferential regimes, FCC could be favored.
\end{itemize}

If this isotropy assumption is relaxed, **FCC or other structures** could emerge, and the universality class would break down.

\paragraph{(C) Strict Locality of the Quartic Vertex}
The **locality of the quartic vertex** is essential to maintaining the isotropic and momentum-independent behavior of the theory. In particular:

\begin{itemize}
\item The quartic vertex must be **momentum-independent**.
\item If the **microscopic model** introduces angle dependence (e.g., due to interactions that break local isotropy), this would generate **new structure-specific weights**.
\item Such an interaction would break the universality class and lead to possible **new structure selection mechanisms**.
\end{itemize}

Thus, any departure from local, momentum-independent quartic vertices leads to a breakdown in the universality of BCC/FCC/SC selection.

\paragraph{(D) Single-Shell Dominance}
The **single-shell assumption** simplifies the model by assuming that only one shell participates in structure formation:

\begin{itemize}
\item In real physical systems, there could be **multi-shell coupling**.
\item **Higher-harmonic generation** and **anisotropic dispersion** effects may also be present.
\item These effects could introduce **new combinatorial weights** beyond the simple pair-sum counting used in the current model.
\end{itemize}

Relaxation of this assumption could lead to a more complex resonance structure, requiring new weights to be considered in the universality class.

\paragraph{(E) No Cubic Term (Z\(_2\) Symmetry)}
The model assumes **Z\(_2\) symmetry**, which forbids cubic terms in the potential:

\begin{itemize}
\item If **cubic terms** are introduced, a well-known mechanism favors FCC over BCC.
\item **Cubic terms** would **strongly enhance FCC** and disrupt the BCC/FCC/SC ordering.
\end{itemize}

Thus, if cubic terms are allowed, the universality of the BCC selection is compromised.

\paragraph{(F) No Lattice Pinning / No External Field}
The current model assumes there is no external field or lattice potential pinning:

\begin{itemize}
\item In the presence of external potentials (e.g., substrate effects, confinement), structures outside of BCC/FCC/SC may emerge.
\item **Combinatorial universality** would be broken, and new structural configurations could dominate the phase selection.
\end{itemize}

Thus, the **absence of lattice pinning** is essential for maintaining the universality of BCC/FCC/SC selection.

\subsection{2.3 Summary of Remaining Assumptions and Potential Breakdowns}

The model presented here is **strongly closed** under the assumptions listed in Section 2.2. However, should any of these assumptions break, **new weight types** and **new structures** could emerge. Specifically:

- **Equal-amplitude ansatz** allows simplification but could break down under amplitude variation.
- **Isotropy and locality** assumptions are central; relaxing them could favor different structures (e.g., FCC).
- **Single-shell approximation** may be invalidated by multi-shell effects and higher harmonics.
- The **absence of cubic terms** and **no external field** are assumptions that, when violated, introduce new selection mechanisms.

\begin{remark}
Breaking these assumptions introduces new structural weights, and **Universality C–E** would need to be revisited in these extended scenarios.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Generalized universality after assumption relaxations]
When any of the hidden assumptions (A)–(F) are relaxed, the universality class can break, and new combinatorial weights (e.g., angular moments, harmonic weights, higher-order invariants) must be introduced to describe the structure selection mechanism.
\end{theorem}
